 CHAPTER XXXI 

 ADRIFT 

 have get money enough to last long with one bring up to simplicity , and resolve to have nothing to do for a while with charity or furnish lodging   what though keep by one 's own nurse , I cast about now for good reason to be off from all the busy work at Bruntsea . so soon after such a tremendous blow , it be impossible for I to push my own little trouble and concern upon good Mr. Shovelin 's family , much as I long to know what be to become of my father 's will , if any thing . but my desire to be do something , or , at least , to get away for a time from Bruntsea , be largely increase by Sir Montague Hockin 's strange behavior toward I . 
 that young man , if still he could be call young which , at my age , scarcely seem to be his right , for he must have be ten year old than poor Firm begin more and more every day to come after I , just when I want to be quite alone . there be nothing more soothing to my thought and mind   the latter get quiet from the former , I suppose   than for the whole of I to rest a while in such a little scollop of the shingle as a new - moon tide , in little crescent , leave just below high - water mark . and now it be new - moon tide again , a fortnight after the flooding of our fly by the activity of the full moon ; and , feel how I long to understand these thing which seem to be deny to all who be of the same sex as the moon herself I sit in a very nice nick , where no wind could make I look bad than nature will . but of my own look I never do think twice , unless there be any one to speak of such a subject .

 I never receive he with bad grace , often as I have repulse he ; but he be one of those people who think that woman be all whim and way .

 this gentleman look at I in a way which I have often observe in England . a general idea there prevail that the free and enlighten native of the West be in front of those here in intelligence , and to some extent , therefore , in dishonesty . but there must be many case where the two be not the same .

" in such a case , the last thing that you should do be that . think only of what be right and honorable , and your duty toward a lady . also your duty to the law of your country .  dislike that gentleman more and more for his supercilious conduct and certainty of subdue I , I naturally turn again to my good host and hostess . but here there be very little help or support to be obtain at present . major Hockin be lay the foundation of " The Bruntsea Assembly - Rooms , Literary Institute , Mutual Improvement Association , Lyceum , and Baths , from sixpence upward ; " while Mrs. Hockin have a hatch of " White Sultans , " or , rather , a prolong sitting of egg , fondly hop to hatch at last , from have cost so much , like a chicken - hearted conference . much as I sorrow at her disappointment for the sit cost twelve guineas I could not feel quite guiltless of a petty and ignoble smile , when , after hop against hope , upon the thirtieth day she place she beautifully sound egg in a large bowl of warm water , in which they float as calmly as if their price be a penny a dozen . the poor lady try to believe that they be spin with vitality ; but at last she allow I to break one , and lo ! it have be half boil by the advertiser . " this be very sad , " cry Mrs. Hockin ; and the patient old hen , who be come in a basket of hay to see the end of it , echo with a cluck that sentiment .

I pay he for my board and lodging , upon a scale settle by Uncle Sam himself , at California price ; therefore I be under no obligation to conceal his foible . but , take he altogether , he be good and brave and just , though unable , from absence of inner light , to be to I what Uncle Sam have be . 
 when I perceive that the Major condemn my simple behavior in London , and   if I may speak it , as I say it to myself   " blow hot and cold " in half a minute hot when I think of any good thing to be do , and cold as soon as he become the man to do they also , when I remember what a chronic plague be now at Bruntsea , in the shape of Sir Montague , who go to and fro , but could never be trust to be far off , I resolve to do what I have long be think of , and believe that my guardian , if he have live another day , would have recommend . I resolve to go and see Lord Castlewood , my father 's first cousin and friend in need . 
 when I ask my host and hostess what they think of this , they both declare that it be the very thing they be at the point of advising , which , however , they have forbear from do because I never take advice . at this , as be such a great exaggeration , I could not help smile seriously ; but I could not accept their sage opinion that , before I go to see my kinsman , I ought to write and ask his leave to do so . for that would have make it quite a rude thing to call , as I must still have do , if he should decline beforehand to receive I . moreover , it would look as if I seek an invitation , while only want an interview . therefore , be now full of money again , I hire the flyman who have make we taste the water , and take train at Newport , and change at two or three place as order , cross many little stream , and come to a fair river , which prove to be the Thames itself , a few mile above read . 
 in spite of all the large lesson of travel , adventure , and tribulation , my heart be throb with some rather small feeling , as for the first time I draw near to the home of my forefather . I should have be sorry to find it ugly or mean , or lie in a hole , or even modern or insignificant ; and when none of these charge could be bring against it , I be fill with highly discreditable pain that Providence have not see fit to issue I into this world in the masculine form ; in which case this fine property would , accord to the rule of mankind , have be mine . however , I be very soon ashamed of such idea , and sit down on a bank to dispel they with the free and fair view around I . 
 the builder of that house know well both where to place and how to shape it , so as not to spoil the site . it stand near the brow of a bosom hill , which shelter it , both with wood and clevice , from the rigor and fury of the north and east ; while in front the slop foreground widen its soft lap of green . in bay and wave of roll grass , promontorie , here and there , by jut copse or massive tree , and jot now and then with cattle as calm as boat at anchor , the range of sunny upland fall to the reedy fringe and cluster silence of deep river meadow . here the Thames , in pleasant bend of gentleness and courtesy , yet with will of its own way , be now a plenteous river , spread low music , and hold mirror to the wood and hill and field , cast afar a broad still gleam , and on the bank present tremulous infinitude of flash . 
 now these thing touch I all the more because none of they belong to I ; and , after thus try to enlarge my view , I get up with much well heart , and hurry on to have it over , whatever it might be . a girl bring up in the real english way would have spend she last shilling to drive up to the door in the fly at the station a most sad machine but I think it no disgrace to go in a more becoming manner . 
 one scarcely ever act up to the force of situation ; and I go as quietly into that house as if it be Betsy Bowen 's . if any body have be rude to I , or ask who I be , or a little thing of that sort , my spirit might have be up at once , and find , as usually happen then , good reason to go down afterward . but happily there be nothing of the kind . an elderly man , without any gaudy badge , open the door very quietly , and beg my pardon , before I speak , for ask I to speak softly . it be one of his lordship 's very bad day , and when he be so , every sound seem to reach he . I take the hint , and do not speak at all , but follow he over deep matting into a little room to which he show I . and then I give he a little note , write before I leave Bruntsea , and ask he whether he think that his master be well enough to attend to it . 
 he look at I in a peculiar manner , for he have know my father well , have serve from his youth in the family ; but he only ask whether my message be important . I answer that it be , but that I would wait for another time rather than do any harm . but he say that , however ill his master be , nothing provoke he more than to find that any thing be neglect through it . and before I could speak again he be go with my letter to Lord Castlewood . 




 CHAPTER XXXII 

 AT HOME 

 some of the miserable , and I might say strange , thing which have befall I from time to time unseasonably , now begin to force their remembrance upon I . such dark figure always seem to make the most of a nervous moment , when solid reason yield to flutter fear and small misgiving . there any body seem to lie , as a stranded sailor lie , at the foot of perpendicular cliff of most inhuman humanity , with all the world frown down over the crest , and no one to throw a rope down . often and often have I feel this want of any one to help I , but the only way out of it seem to be to do my good to help myself . 
 even , now I have little hope , have be so often dash , and know that my father 's cousin possess no share of my father 's strength . he might , at the utmost , give good advice , and help I with kind feeling ; but if he want to do more , surely he might have try ere now . but my thought about this be cut short by a message that he would be glad to see I , and I follow the servant to the library . 
 here I find Lord Castlewood sit in a high - back chair , uncushioned and uncomfortable . when he see I near he he get up and take my hand , and look at I , and I be pleased to find his face well - mean , brave , and generous . but even to rise from his chair be plainly no small effort to he , and he lean upon a staff or crutch as he offer I a small white hand . 
 " Miss Castlewood , " he say , with a very weak yet clear and silvery voice , " for many year I have long in vain and seek in vain to hear of you . I have not escape all self - reproach through my sense of want of energy ; yet , such as I be , I have do my good , or I do my good to think so . " 
 " I be sure you have , " I reply , without think , know his kindness to my father , and feel the shame of my own hot word to Mr. Shovelin about he . " I owe you more gratitude than I can tell , for your goodness to my dear father . I be not come now to trouble you , but because it be my duty . " 
 while I be speak he manage to lead I , feebly as himself could walk , to a deep chair for reading , or some such use , whereof I have have few chance . and in every step and word and gesture I recognize that foreign grace which true - bear briton be proud to despise on both side of the Atlantic . and , be in the light , I watch he well , because I be not a foreigner . 
 in the clear summer light of the westere sun   which be well for accurate us than the radiance of the morning   I see a firm , calm face , which might in good health have be powerful a face which might be call the moonlight image of my father 's . I could not help turn away to cry , and suspicion flee forever . 
 " my dear young cousin , " he say , as soon as I be fit to speak to , " your father trust I , and so must you . you may think that I have forget you , or do very little to find you out . it be no indifference , no forgetfulness : I have not be able to work myself , and I have have very deep trouble of my own . " 
 he lean on his staff , and look down at I , for I have sit down when thus overcome , and I know that the forehead and eye be those of a learn and intellectual man . how I know this it be impossible to say , for I never have meet with such a character as this , unless it be the Abbe of Flechon , when I be only fourteen year old , and value his great skill in spin a top tenfold more than all his deep learning . Lord Castlewood have long , silky hair , fall in curl of silver gray upon either side of his beautiful forehead , and the gaze of his soft dark eye be sad , gentle , yet penetrate . weak health and almost constant pain have chasten his delicate feature to an expression almost feminine , though firm thin lip and rigid line show masculine will and fortitude . and when he speak of his own trouble   which , perhaps , he would not have do except for consolation 's sake , I know that he mean something even more grievous than bodily anguish . 
 " it be hard , " he say , " that you , so young and healthy and full of high spirit as you be   unless your face belie you , should begin the good year of your life , as common opinion put such thing , in such a cloud of gloom and shame . " 
 " there be no shame at all , " I answer ; " and if there be gloom , I be use to that ; and so be my father for year and year . what be my trouble compare with his ? " 
 " your trouble be nothing when compare with his , so far as regard the mere weight of it ; but he be a strong man to carry his load ; you be a young and a sensitive woman . the burden may even be bad for you . now tell I all about yourself , and what have bring you to I . " 
 his voice be so quiet and soothing that I seem to rest beneath it . he have not speak once of religion or the will of God , nor ply I at all with those pious allusion , which even to the reverent mind be like illusion when so urge . Lord Castlewood have too deep a sense of the will of God to know what it be ; and he look at I wistfully as at one who might have bad experience of it . 
 fall happily under his influence , as his clear , kind eye meet mine , I tell he every thing I could think of about my father and myself , and all I want to do next , and how my heart and soul be set upon get to the bottom of every thing . and while I speak with spirit , or softness , or , I fear , sometimes with hate , I could not help see that he be surprised , but not wholly displeased , with my energy . and then , when all be exhaust , come the old question I have hear so often , and find so hard to answer 
 " and what do you propose to do next , Erema ? " 
 " to go to the very place itself , " I say , speak strongly under challenge , though quite unresolved about such a thing before ; " to live in the house where my father live , and my mother and all of the family die ; and from day to day to search every corner and fish up every bit of evidence , until I get hold of the true man at last , of the villain who do it who do it , and leave my father and all the rest of we to be condemn and die for it . " 
 " Erema , " reply my cousin , as he have tell I now to call he , " you be too impetuous for such work , and it be wholly unfit for you . for such a task , person of train sagacity and keen observation be need . and after all these eighteen year , or nearly nineteen now it must be , there can not be any thing to discover there . " 
 " but if I like , may I go there , cousin , if only to satisfy my own mind ? I be miserable now at Bruntsea , and Sir Montague Hockin wear I out . " 
 " Sir Montague Hockin ! " Lord Castlewood exclaim ; " why , you do not tell I that he be there . wherever he be , you should not be . " 
 " I forgot to speak of he . he do not live there , but be continually to and fro for bathing , or fishing , or rabbit - shooting , or any other pretext . and he make the place very unpleasant to I , kind as the Major and Mrs. Hockin be , because I can never make he out at all . " 
 " do not try to do so , " my cousin answer , look at I earnestly ; " be content to know nothing of he , my dear . if you can put up with a very dull house , and a host who be even dull , come here and live with I , as your father would have wish , and as I , your near relative , now ask and beg of you . " 
 this be wonderfully kind , and for a moment I feel tempt . Lord Castlewood be an elderly man , and , as the head of our family , my natural protector , there could be nothing wrong , and there might be much that be good , in such an easy arrangement . but , on the other hand , it seem to I that after this my work would languish . live in comfort and prosperity under the roof of my forefather , beyond any doubt I should begin to fall into habit of luxury , to take to the love of literature , which I know to be latent within I , to lose the clear , strong , practical sense of the duty for which I , the last of seven , be spar , and in some measure , perhaps , by wandering and by hardship , fit . and then I think of my host 's weak health , continual pain   the sign of which be hardly repress even while he be speak , and probably also his secluded life . be it fair to force he , by virtue of his inborn kindness and courtesy , to come out of his privilege and deal with I , who could not altogether be in any place a mere nobody ? and so I refuse his offer . 
 " I be very much oblige to you indeed , " I say , " but I think you might be sorry for it . I will come and stop with you every now and then , when your health be well , and you ask I . but to live here altogether would not do ; I should like it too well , and do nothing else . " 
 " perhaps you be right , " he reply , with the air of one who care little for any thing , which be to I the most melancholy thing , and bad than any distress almost ; " you be very young , my dear , and year should be allow to pass before you know what full - grown sorrow be . you have have enough , for your age , of it . you have well not live in this house ; it be not a house for cheerfulness . " 
 " then if I must neither live here nor at Bruntsea , " I ask , with sudden remonstrance , feel as if every body desire to be quit of I or to worry I , " to what place in all the world be I to go , unless it be back to America ? I will go at once to Shoxford , and take lodging of my own . " 
 " perhaps you have well wait a little while , " Lord Castlewood answer , gently , " although I would much rather have you at Shoxford than where you be at present . but please to remember , my good Erema , that you can not go to Shoxford all alone . I have a most faithful and trusty man the one who open the door to you . he have be here before his remembrance . he disdain I still as compare with your father . will you have he to superintend you ? I scarcely see how you can do any good , but if you do go , you must go openly , and as your father 's daughter . " 
 " I have no intention whatever of go in any other way , Lord Castlewood ; but perhaps , " I continue , " it would be as well to make as little stir as possible . of an english village I know nothing but the little I have see at Bruntsea , but there they make a very great fuss about any one who come down with a man - servant . " 
 " to be sure , " reply my cousin , with a smile ; " they would not be true briton otherwise . perhaps you would do well without Stixon ; but of course you must not go alone . could you by any mean persuade your old nurse Betsy to go with you ? " 
 " how good of you to think of it ! how wise you be ! " I really could not help say , as I gaze at his delicate and noble face . " I be sure that if Betsy can come , she will ; though of course she must be compensate well for the waste all her lodger will make of it . they be very wicked , and eat most dreadfully if she even take one day 's holiday . what do you think they even do ? she have tell I with tear in her eye of it . they be all allow a pat of butter , a penny roll , and two sardine for breakfast . no soon do they know that her back be turn " 
 " Erema ! " cry my cousin , with some surprise ; and be so recalled , I be ashamed . but I never could help take interest in very little thing indeed , until my own common - sense , or somebody else , come to tell I what a child I be . however , I do believe that Uncle Sam like I all the well for this fault . 
 " my dear , I do not mean to blame you , " Lord Castlewood say , most kindly ; " it must be a great relief for you to look on at other people . but tell I or rather , since you have tell I almost every thing you know let I , if only in one way I can help you , help you at least in that way . " 
 know that he must mean money , I decline , from no false pride , but a set resolve to work out my work , if possible , through my own resource . but I promise to apply to he at once if scarcity should again befall I , as have happen lately . and then I long to ask he why he seem to have so low an opinion of Sir Montague Hockin . that question , however , I fear to put , because it might not be a proper one , and also because my cousin have speak in a very strange tone , as if of some private dislike or reserve on that subject . moreover , it be too evident that I have try his courtesy long enough . from time to time pale shade of bodily pain , and then hot flush , have flit across his face , like cloud on a windy summer evening . and more than once he have glance at the time - piece , not to hurry I , but as if he dread its announcement . it be a beautiful clock , and strike with a silvery sound every quarter of an hour . and now , as I rise to say good - by , to catch my evening train , it strike a quarter to five , and my cousin stand up , with his weight upon his staff , and look at I with an inexpressible depth of weary misery . 
 " I have only a few minute leave , " he say , " during which I can say any thing . my time be divide into two sad part : the time when I be capable of very little , and the time when I be capable of nothing ; and the latter part be twice the length of the other . for sixteen hour of every day , far well have I be dead than live , so far as our own little insolence may judge . but I speak of it only to excuse bad manner , and perhaps I show bad by do so . I shall not be able to see you again until to - morrow morning . do not go ; they will arrange all that . send a note to major Hockin by Stixon 's boy . Stixon and Mrs. Price will see to your comfort , if those who be free from pain require any other comfort . forgive I ; I do not mean to be rude . sometimes I can not help give way . " 
 less enviable than the poor slave , Lord Castlewood sink upon his hard stiff chair , and straighten his long narrow hand upon his knee , and set his thin lip in straight blue line . each hand be as rigid as the ivory handle of an umbrella or walking - stick , and his lip be like clamp wire . this be his regular way of prepare for the onset of the night , so that no grimace , no cry , no moan , or other token of fierce agony should be wring from he . 
 " my lord will catch it stiff to - night , " say Mr. Stixon , who come as I ring , and then lead I away to the drawing - room ; " he always have it ten time bad after any talking or any thing to upset he like . and so , then , miss excuse a humble servant do I understand from he that you be the Captain 's own daughter ? " 
 " yes ; but surely your master want you he be in such dreadful pain . do please to go to he , and do something . " 
 " there be nothing to be do , miss , " Stixon answer , with calm resignation ; " he be bind to stay so for sixteen hour , and then he ease off again . but bless my heart , miss excuse I in your presence his lordship be thoroughly use to it . it be my certain knowledge that for seven year now he have never have seven minute free from pain seven minute all of a heap , I mean . some do say , miss , as the Lord doeth every thing accord to his righteousness , that the reason be not very far to seek . " 
 I ask he what he mean , though I ought , perhaps , to have put a stop to his loquacity ; and he pretend not to hear , which make I ask he all the more . 
 " a well man never live than my lord , " he answer , with a little shock at my misprision ; " but it have be say among censoorous person that nobody ever have no luck as come in suddenly to a property and a high state of life on the top of the head of a family of seven . " 
 " what a poor superstition ! " I cry , though I be not quite sure of its be a wicked one . " but what be your master 's malady , Stixon ? surely there might be something do to relieve his violent pain , even if there be no real cure for it ? " 
 " no , miss , nothing can be do . the doctor have exorce themselves . they try this , that , and the other , but nature only fly bad against they . ' tis a thing as be never hear of till the Constitooshon be knock on the head and to piece by the Reform Bill . and though they could not   cure it , they do what they could do , miss . they discover a very good name for it they christen it the ' new - rager ! ' " 




 CHAPTER XXXIII 

 LORD castlewood 

 in the morning , when I be call again to see my afflict cousin Stixon junior have gladly go to explain thing for I at Bruntsea little as I know of any bodily pain   except hunger , or thirst , or weariness , and once in my life a headache , I stand before Lord Castlewood with a deference and humility such as I have never feel before toward any human being . not only because he bear perpetual pain in the two degree of night and day the day be dark and the night jet - black without a murmur or an evil word ; not only because through the whole of this he have keep his mind clear and his love of knowledge bright ; not even because he have manage , like Job , to love God through the whole of it . all these be good reason for very great and very high respect of any man ; and when there be no claim whatever on his part to any such feeling , it need must come . but when I learn another thing , high respect at once become what might be call deep reverence . and this come to pass in a simple and , as any one must confess , quite inevitable way . 
 it be not to be suppose that I could sit the whole of my first evening in that house without a soul to speak to . so far as my dignity and sense of right permit , I wear out Mr. Stixon , so far as he would go , not ask he any thing that the very worst - minded person could call " inquisitive , " but allow he to talk , as he seem to like to do , while he wait upon I , and alternately lament my hapless history and my hopeless want of taste . 
 " ah , your father , the Captain , now , he would have know what this be ! you have   no right to his eye , Miss Erma , without his tongue and palate . no more of this , miss ! and do for you a - purpose ! well , cook will be put out , and no mistake ! I well not let she see it go down , anyhow . " and the worthy man tearfully put some dainty by , perhaps without any view to his own supper . 
 " Lord Castlewood speak to I about a Mrs. Price the housekeeper , be she not ? " I ask at last , be so accustomed to like what I could get , that the number of dish weary I . 
 " oh yes , miss , " say Stixon , very shortly , as if that description exhaust Mrs. Price . 
 " if she be not too busy , I should like to see she as soon as these thing be all take away . I mean if she be not a stranger , and if she would like to see I . " 
 " no new - comer here , " Mr. Stixon reply ; " we all work our way up regular , the same as my lad be begin for to do . new - fangle way be not accept here . we put the reform spirit scrub of the step till their knuckle be crack and their knee like a bean . the old lord be the man for discipline your grandfather , if you please , miss . he catch I when I be about that high " 
 " excuse I , Mr. Stixon ; but would he have encourage you to talk as you so very kindly talk to I , instead of answer a question ? " 
 I think that poor Stixon would have be upset by this , and be angry with myself for say it ; but instead of be hurt , he only smile and touch his forehead . 
 " well , now , you do remind I uncommon of he then , miss . I could have hear the old lord speak almost , though he be always harsh and distant . and as I be go for to say , he catch I fifty year agone next Lammas - tide ; a pear - tree of an early sort it be ; you may see the very tree if you please to stand here , miss , though the pear be quite alter now , and scarcely fit to eat . well , I be run off with my cap chock - full , miss "

 " oh no , Miss Erma , no reason at all . only please to bear in mind , miss , that she be a coorous woman . she be that jealous , and I might say forward " 
 " then she be capable of speak for herself . " 
 " you be right , miss , there , and no mistake . she can speak for herself and for fifty other word enough , I mean , for all of they . but I would not have she know for all the world that I say it . " 
 " then if you do not send she to I at once , the first thing I shall do will be to tell she . "

 I assure he that my threat be not in earnest , but of pure impatience . and have no motive but downright jealousy for keep Mrs. Price from I , he make up his mind at last to let she come . but he tell I to be careful what I say ; I must not expect it to be at all like talk to himself , for instance . 
 the housekeeper come up at last , by dint of my persistence , and she stop in the doorway and make I a courtesy , which put I out of countenance , for nobody ever do that in America , and scarcely any one in England now , except in country - dancing . instead of be as describe by Stixon , Mrs. Price be of a very quiet , sensible , and respectful kind . she be rather short , but look rather tall , from she even walk and way of carry her head . her figure be neat , and her face clear - speak , with straight pretty eyebrow , and calm bright eye . I feel that I could tell she almost any thing , and she would think before she talk of it . and in my strong want of some woman to advise with Betsy Bowen be very good but very narrow , and Mrs. Hockin a mere echo of the Major until he contradict she , and Suan Isco , with her fine , large view , five thousand mile out of sight just now this be a state of thing to enhance the value of any good countenance feminine . 
 at any rate , I be so glad to see she that , be still ungraduate in the step of rank   though begin to like a good footing there , I run up and take she by both hand , and fetch she out of her grand courtesy and into a low chair . at this she be surprised , as one quick glance show ; and she think I , perhaps , what be call in England " an impulsive creature . " this put I again upon my dignity , for I never have be in any way like that , and I clearly perceive that she ought to understand a little more distinctly my character . 
 it be easy to begin with this intention , but very hard indeed to keep it up when any body of nice way and look be sit with a proper deferential power of listening , and like one 's young idea , which multiply and magnify themselves at each demand . so after some general talk about the weather , the country , the house , and so on , we come to the people of the house , or at any rate the chief person . and I ask she a few quiet question about Lord Castlewood 's health and habit , and any thing else she might like to tell I . for many thing have seem to I a little strange and out of the usual course , and on that account worthy to be speak of without common curiosity . I tell she that this be not satisfactory , and I might say quite the opposite ; that have " allude " to whatever it might be , she be bind to tell I all about it . that I have live in very many country , in all of which wrong thing continually go on , of which I continually hear just in that sort of way and no more . enough to make one uncomfortable , but not enough to keep one instructed and vigilant as to thing that ought to be avoid . upon this she yield either to my argument or to her own dislike of unreasonable silence , and give I the follow account of the misfortune of Lord Castlewood : 
 Herbert William Castlewood be the third son of Dean Castlewood , a young brother of my grandfather , and be bear in the year 1806 . he be old , therefore , than my father , but still   even before my father 's birth , which provide a direct heir   there be many life betwixt he and the family estate . and his father , have as yet no promotion in the Church , find it hard to bring up his child . the eld son get a commission in the army , and the second enter the navy , while Herbert be place in a bank at Bristol not at all the sort of life which he would have choose . but be of a gentle , unselfish nature , as well as a weak constitution , he put up with his state in life , and do his good to give satisfaction . 
 this calm courage generally have its reward , and in the year 1842 , not very long before the death of my grandfather at Shoxford , Mr. Herbert Castlewood , be well - connect , well - behave , diligent , and pleasing , obtain a partnership in the firm , which be , perhaps , the foremost in the west of England . his two eld brother happen then to be at home , Major and Commander Castlewood , each of whom have see very hard service , and find it still hard slavery to make both end meet , although bachelor . but , return full of glory , they find one thing hard still , and that be to extract any cash from their father , the highly venerate Dean , who in that respect , if in no other , very closely resemble the head of the family . therefore these brave man resolve to go and see their Bristol brother , to whom they be tenderly attach , and who now must have money enough and to spare . so they write to their brother to meet they on the platform , scarcely believe that they could be there in so short a time from London ; for they never have travel by rail before ; and they set forth in wonderful spirit , and laugh at the strange , giddy rush of the travelling , and make bet with each other about punctual time   for train keep much well time while new , and , as long as they could time it , they keep time to a second . but , sad to relate , they want no chronometer when they arrive at Bristol , both be kill at a blow , with their watch still go , and a smile on their face . for the train have run into a wall of Bath stone , and several of the passenger be kill . 
 the sight of his two brother carry out like this , after so many year of not see they , be too much for Mr. Herbert Castlewood 's nerve , which always have be delicate . and he shiver all the more from reproach of conscience , have make up his mind not to lend they any money , as a practical banker be compel to do . and from that very moment he begin to feel great pain .

 now I wonder and eagerly yearn to know whether my father , at such a time , and in such a state of loneliness , might not have be lead to impart to his cousin and host and protector the dark mystery which lie at the bottom of his own conduct . know how resolute and stern he be , and doubtless then imbittere by the wreck of love and life , I think it more probable that he have keep silence even toward so near a relative , especially as he have see very little of his cousin Herbert till he have find he thus . moreover , my grandfather and the Dean have spend little brotherly love on each other , have have a life - long feud about a copy - hold furze brake of nearly three - quarter of an acre , as Betsy remember to have hear her master say . 
 to go on , however , with what Mrs. Price be say . she know scarcely any thing about my father , because she be too young at that time to be call into the counsel of the servant ' hall , for she scarcely be thirty - five yet , as she declare , and she certainly do not look forty . but all about the present Lord Castlewood she know well than any body else , perhaps , because she have be in the service of his wife , and , indeed , her chief attendant . then , have speak of her master 's wife , Mrs. Price catch herself up , and thenceforth call she only his " lady .  Mrs. Price , be a very steady person , with a limited income , and enough to do , be inclined to look down upon the state of mind in which Mr. Castlewood become involve . she be not there at the moment , of course , but suddenly send for when all be settle ; nevertheless , she find out afterward how it begin from her master 's man , through what he have for dinner . and in the kitchen - garden at Castlewood no rampion would she allow while she live .  it be out of my power to describe such thing , even if it be my duty to do so , which , happily , it have never be ; moreover , Mrs. Price , in what she tell I , exercise a just and strict reserve . enough that Mr. Castlewood 's wed life be do with in six month and three day .  this have happen about twelve year back , soon after I be place at the school in Languedoc , to which I be take so early in life that I almost forget all about it . but it might have be well for poor Flittamore if she have be bring up at a steady place like that , with sister and lady of retreat , to teach she the proper description of her duty to mankind . I seem now in my own mind to condemn she quite enough , feel how superior her husband must have be ; but Mrs. Price go even far , and become quite indignant that any one should pity she . 
 " a hussy ! a hussy ! a poppet of a hussy ! " she exclaim , with great power than her quiet face could indicate ; " never would I look at she . speak never so , Miss Castlewood . my lord be the very good of all man , and she have make he what he be . the pity she deserve be to be tread under foot , as I see they do in Naples . " 
 after all the passion I have see among rough people , I scarcely could help tremble at the depth of wrath dissemble and firmly control in calm clear eye under very steadfast eyebrow . it be plain that Lord Castlewood have , at any rate , the gift of be love by his dependent . 
 " I hope that he take it aright ! " I cry , catch some of her indignation ; " I hope that he cast she to the wind , without even a sigh for such a cruel creature ! " 
 " he be not strong enough , " she answer , sadly ; " his bodily health be not equal to it . from childhood he have be partly crippled and spoil in his nerve by an accident . and the shock of that sight at Bristol fly to his weakness , and be too much for he . and now this third and bad disaster , come upon he where his good hope lay , and at such a time of life , take he altogether off his leg . and off his head too , I might almost say , miss ; for , instead of blame she , he put the fault entirely upon himself . at his time of life , and in such poor health , he should not have marry a bright young girl : how could he ever hope to make she happy ? that be how he look at it , when he should have send constable after she . " 
 " and what become of she the mindless animal , to forsake so good and great a man ! I do hope she be punish , and that vile man too . " 
 " she be , Miss Castlewood ; but he be not ; at least he have not receive justice yet . but he will , he will , he will , miss . the treacherous thief ! and my lord receive he as a young fellow - countryman under a cloud , and lend he money , and save he from starve ; for he have break with his father and be run from his creditor . " 
 " tell I no more , " I say ; " not another word . it be my fate to meet that well , that gentleman almost every day . and he , and he oh , how thankful I be to have find out all this about he ! " 
 the above will show why , when I meet my father 's cousin on the follow morning with his grand , calm face , as benevolent as if he have pass a night of luxurious rest instead of sleepless agony I know myself to be of a low order in mind and soul and heart than his ; a small , narrow , passionate girl , in the presence of a large , broad - sighted , and compassionate man . 
 I throw myself altogether on his will ; for , when I trust , I trust wholly . and , under his advice , I do not return with any rash haste to Bruntsea , but write in discharge of all duty there ; while Mrs. Price , a clear and steadfast woman , be send to London to see Wilhelmina Strouss . these two must have have very great talk together , and , both be zealous and faithful , they come to many misunderstanding . however , on the whole , they become very honest friend , and swear ally at last , discover more , the more they talk , people against whom they feel a common and just enmity . 




 CHAPTER XXXIV 

 SHOXFORD 

 be there people who have never , in the course of anxious life , feel desire to be away , to fly away , from every thing , however good and dear to they , and rest a little , and think new thought , or let new thought flow into they , from the gentle air of some new place , where nobody have hear of they a place whose care , be feel by proxy , almost seem romantic , and where the eye spare brain and heart with a critic 's self - complacence ? if any such place yet remain , the happy soul may seek it in an inland english village . 
 a village where no billow be to stun or to confound it , no crag or precipice to trouble it with giddiness , and where no hurry of restless tide make time , its own father , uneasy . but in the quiet , at the bottom of the valley , a beautiful rivulet , belong to the place , hasten or linger , accord to its mood ; hanker here and there , not to be away yet ; and then , by the doing of its own work , lead to a swift perplexity of ripple . here along its side , and there softly lean over it , fresh green meadow lie repose in the settled meaning of the summer day . for this be a safe time of year than the flourish of the spring - tide , when the impulse of young warmth awake be suddenly smite by the bleak east wind , and cowslip and cuckoo - flower and speedwell get their bright lip brown with cold . then , moreover , must the mead have feel the worry of scarcely know yet what would be demand of they ; whether to carry an exact load of hay , or only to feed a few saunter cow . 
 but now every trouble have be settle for the good ; the long grass be mown , and the short grass browse , and caper of the fairy and caprice of the cow have dapple wear texture with a deep green . therefore let eye that be satisfied here as any but a very bad eye must be , with so many change of softness follow the sweet lead of the valley ; and there , in a bend of the gently brawl river , stand the never - brawl church . 
 a church less trouble with the gift of tongue be not to be find in England : a church of gray stone that crumble just enough to entice frail mortal sympathy , and confess to the storm it have undergo in a tone that conciliate the human sigh . the tower be large , and high enough to tell what the way of the wind be without any potato - bury on the top , and the simple roof be not cruciate with tile of misguided fancy . but gray rest , and peace of age , and content of lie calmly six foot deep than the bustle of the quick ; memory also , and oblivion , follow each other slowly , like the shadow of the church - yard tree for all of these no well place can be , nor soft comfort . 
 for the village of Shoxford run up on the rise , and straggle away from its burial - place , as a child from his school go mitche . there be some few little up and down in the manner of its building , as well as in other particular about it ; but still it keep as parallel with the crooked river as the far more crooked way of man permit . but the whole of the little road of house run down the valley from the church - yard gate ; and above the church , look up the pretty valley , stand nothing but the mill and the plank bridge below it ; and a furlong above that again the stone bridge , where the main road cross the stream , and be console by lead to a big house the Moonstock Inn . 
 the house in which my father live so long or rather , I should say , my mother , while he be away with his regiment and where we unfortunate seven see the light , stand about half - way down the little village , be on the right - hand side of the road as you come down the valley from the Moonstock bridge . therefore it be on the further and upper side of the street if it can be call a street from the valley and the river and the mead below the mill , inasmuch as every bit of Shoxford , and every particle of the parish also , have existence of no mean sort , as compare with other parish , in its own esteem on the right side of the river Moon . 
 my father 's house , in this good village , stand endwise to the street , be high at one end than at the other . that be to say , the ground come slop , or even fall , as fairly might be say , from one end to the other of it , so that it look like a Noah 's ark tilt by Behemoth under the stern - post . and a little lane , from a finely woode hill , here fall steeply into the " High Street "   as the grocer and the butcher love to call it , and make my father 's house most distinct , by obey a good deal of its outline , and discharge in heavy rain a free supply of water under the weather - board of our front - door . this front - door open on the little steep triangle form by the meeting of lane and road , while the back - door lead into a long but narrow garden run along the road , but raise some foot above it ; the bank be keep up by a rough stone wall crest with stick - up snap - dragon and valerian , and face with rosette and disk and dill of houseleek , pennywort , and hart' - tongue . 
 Betsy and I be only just in time to see the old house as it use to be ; for the owner have die about half a year ago , and his grandson , have prove his will , be resolve to make short work with it . the poor house be blame for the sorrow it have shelter , and have the repute of two spectre , as well as the pale shadow of misfortune . for my dear father be now believe by the superstitious villager to haunt the old home of his happiness and love , and roam from room to room in search of his wife and all his child . but his phantom be most careful not to face that of his father , which stalk along haughtily , as behoove a lord , and point forever to a red wound in its breast . no wonder , therefore , that the house would never let ; and it would have be pull down long ago if the owner have not feel a liking for it , through memory tender and peculiar to himself . his grandson , have none of these to contend with , resolve to make a mere stable of it , and build a public - house at the bottom of the garden , and turn the space between they into skittle - ground , and so forth . 
 to I this seem such a very low idea , and such a desecration of a sacred spot , that if I have own any money to be sure of , I would have offer hundred to prevent it . but I find myself now in a delicate state of mind concern money , have little of my own , and doubt how much other people might intend for I . so that I durst not offer to buy land and a house without any mean to pay . 
 and it be not for that reason only that Betsy and I keep ourselves quiet . we know that any stir in this little place about we such as my name might at once set go would once for all destroy all hope of do good by come . Betsy know more of such matter than I do , besides all her knowledge of the place itself , and her great superiority of age ; therefore I leave to she all little management , as be in every way fair and wise . for Mrs. Strouss have forsake a large and good company of lodger , with only Herr Strouss to look after they and who be he among they ? if she trod on one side of her foot , or feel a tingling in her hand , or a buzzing in her ear , she know in a moment what it be of pound and pound be she be cheat , a hundred mile off , by foreigner ! 
 for this reason it have cost much persuasion and many appeal to her faithfulness , as well as considerable weekly payment , ere ever my good nurse could be bring away from London ; and perhaps even so she never would have come if I have not write myself to Mrs. Price , then visit Betsy in European Square , that if the landlady be too busy to be spar by her lodger , I must try to get Lord Castlewood to spare I his housekeeper . upon this Mrs. Strouss at once declare that Mrs. Price would ruin every thing ; and rather than that no matter what she lose she herself would go with I . and so she do , and she manage very well , keep my name out of sight   for , happen what might , I would have no false one ; and she get quiet lodging in her present name , which sound nicely foreign ; and the village be more agitated now about my father 's material house , and the work they be promise in pull it down , than about his shattered household , we have a very favorable time for come in , and be pronounce to be foreigner who must not be allow to run up bill . 
 this rustic conclusion suit we quite well , and we soon confirm it unwittingly , Betsy offer a german thaler and I an american dollar at the shop of the village chandler and baker , so that we be look upon with some pity , and yet a kind desire for our custom . thus , without any attempt of our at either delusion or mystery , Mrs. Strouss be hail throughout the place as " Madam Straw , " while I , through the sagacity of a deeply read shoe - maker , obtain a foreign name , as will by - and - by appear . 
 we lodge at the post - office , not through any wisdom or even any thought on our part , but simply because we happen there to find the clean and prettiest room in the place . for the sun be now in the height of August , and have much harvest to ripen , at middle day come ramp down the little street of Shoxford like the chairman of the guild of baker . every house have lately brighten up its whitewash which they always do there when the frost be over , soon after the feast of St. Barnabas and the weed of the way have fare amiss in the absence of any water - cart , it be not in the strong , sharp character of the sun to miss such an opportunity . after the red californian glare , I have no fear of any english sun ; but Betsy be frightened , and both of we be glad to get into a little place shelter by green blind . this chance to be the post - office , and there we find nice lodging . 
 by an equal chance this prove to be the wise thing we could possibly have do , if we have set about it carefully . for why , that nobody ever would impute any desire of secrecy to people who straightway unpacked their box at the very head - quarter of all the village news . and the mistress of the post be a sharp - tongue woman , please to speak freely of her neighbor ' doing , and prompt with good advice that they should heed their own business , if any of they durst say a word about her own . she keep a tidy little shop , show something of almost every thing ; but we have a side door , quite of our own , where Betsy meet the baker 's wife and the veritable milkman ; and neither of they know she , which be just what she have hop ; and yet it make she speak amiss of they . 
 but if all thing must be bring to the harsh test of dry reason , I myself might be hard push to say what good I hop to do by come thus to Shoxford . I know of a great many thing , for certain , that never have be thoroughly examine here ; also I naturally wish to see , be a native , what the native be ; and , much more than that , it be always on my mind that here lie my mother and the other six of we . 
 therefore it be an impatient thing for I to hear Betsy work out the afternoon with perpetual chatter and challenge of price , combat now as a lodger all those point which as a landlady she never would allow even to be moot question . if any applicant in European Square have dare so much as hint at any of all the requirement which she now expect gratis , she would simply have whisk her duster , and say that the lodging for such people must be look for down the alley . however , Mrs. Busk , our new landlady , although she have a temper of her own   as any one keep a post - office must have   be force by the rarity of lodger here to yield many point , which Mrs. Strouss , on her own board , would not even have allow to be debate . all this be entirely against my wish ; for when I have money , I spend it , find really no other good in it ; but Betsy tell I that the pure principle of all be not to be cheat . 
 so I leave she to have these little matter out , and take that occasion for steal away   as the hour grow on toward evening   to a place where I wish to be quite alone . and the shadow of the western hill shed peace upon the valley , when I cross a little stile lead into Shoxford church - yard . 
 for a minute or two I be quite afraid , see nobody any where about , nor even hear any sound in the distance to keep I company . for the church lie apart from the village , and be thickly plant out from it , the live folk be full of superstition , and deeply believe in the dead people 's ghost . and even if this be a wife to a husband , or even a husband reappear to his wife , there be not a man or a woman in the village that would not run away from it . 
 this I do not know at present , not have be there long enough ; neither have I any terror of that sort , not be quite such a coward , I should hope . but still , as the mantle of the cold tree darken , and the stony remembrance of the dead grow pale , and of the living there be not even the whistle of a grave - digger my heart get the well of my mind for a moment , and make I long to be across that stile again . because   as I say to myself   if there have be a hill to go up , that would be so different and so easy ; but go down into a place like this , whence the only escape must be by step , and where any flight must be along channel that run in and out of graf and tombstone , I try not to be afraid , yet could not altogether help it . 
 but lo ! when I come to the north side of the tower , scarcely think what to look for , I find myself in the middle of a place which make I stop and wonder . here be six little grassy tuffet , accord to the length of child , all lay east and west , without any stint of room , harmoniously . 
 from the eld to the young , one could almost tell the age at which their lowly stature stop , and take its final measurement . 
 and in the middle be a large grave , to comfort and encourage they , as a hen lie down among her chick and wait for they to shelter . without a name to any of they , all these seven graf lie together , as in a fairy ring of rest , and kind compassion have prevent any stranger from come to be bury there . 
 I would not sit on my mother 's grave for fear of crush the pretty grass , which some one tend carefully ; but I stand at its foot , and bent my head , and count all the little one . then I think of my father in the grove of peach , more than six thousand mile away , on the bank of the soft Blue River . and a sense of desolate sorrow and of the blessing of death overwhelm I . 




 CHAPTER XXXV 

 the sexton 

 with such thing in my mind , it take I long to come back to my work again . it even seem a wicked thing , so near to all these proof of God 's great visitation over we , to walk about and say , " I will do this , " or even to think , " I will try to do that . " my own poor helplessness , and loss of live love to guide I , lay upon my heart a weight from which it scarcely care to move . all be bury , all be do with , all have pass from out the world , and leave no mark but graf behind . what good to stir anew such sadness , even if a poor weak thing like I could move its mystery ? 
 Time , however , and my nurse Betsy , and Jacob Rigg the gardener , bring I back to a well state of mind , and renew the right courage within I . but , first of all , Jacob Rigg arouse my terror and interest vividly . it may be remember that this good man have be my father 's gardener at the time of our great calamity , and almost alone of the Shoxford people have show himself true and faithful . not that the native have turn against we , or be at all unfriendly ; so far from this be the case , that every one feel for our trouble , and pity we , my father be of a cheerful and affable turn , until misery harden he ; but what I mean be that only one or two have the courage to go against the popular conclusion and the conviction of authority .

 CHAPTER xxxvi 

 a simple question 

 now this account of what Jacob Rigg have see and hear throw I into a state of mind extremely unsatisfactory . to be in eager search of some unknown person who have injure I inexpressibly , without any longing for revenge on my part , but simply with a view to justice this be a very different thing from feel that an unknown person be in quest of I , with the horrible purpose of destroy I to insure his own wicked safety . 
 at first I almost think that he be welcome to do this ; that such a life as mine   if look at from an outer point of view   be well to be die than live out . also that there be nobody leave to get any good out of all that I could do ; and even if I ever should succeed , truth would come out of her tomb too late . and this begin to make I cry , which I have long give over do , with no one to feel for the heart of it . 
 but a thing of this kind could not long endure ; and as soon as the sun of the morrow arise   or at least as soon as I be fit to see he , my view of the world be quite different . here be the merry brook , play with the morning , spread around with ample depth and rich retreat of meadow , and often , after maze of leisure , hasten with a tinkle into shadowy delight of tree . here , as well , be happy lane , and footpath of a soft content , unworn with any pressure of the price of time or business . none of they know   in spite , at flurry spot , of their own direction post   whence they be come or whither go only that here they lay , between the field or through they , like idle vein of earth , with sometimes company of a man or boy , whistle to his footfall , or a singing maid with a milk pail . and how ungrateful it would be to forget the pleasant copse , in wave of deep green leafage flow down and up the channel hill , wave at the wind to tint and tone of new refreshment , and tempt idle folk to come and hear the hush , and see the twinkled texture of pellucid gloom . 
 much , however , as I love to sit in place of this kind alone , for some little time I fear to do so , after hear the sexton 's tale ; for Jacob 's terror be so unfeigned   though his own life have not be threaten   that , know as I do from Betsy 's account , as well as his own appearance , that he be not at all a nervous man , I could not help share his vague alarm . it seem so terrible that any one should come to the graf of my sweet mother and her six harmless child , and , instead of show pity , as even a monster might have try to do , should stand , if not with threaten gesture , yet with a most hostile mien , and thirst for the life of the only survivor my poor self . 
 but terrible or not , the truth be so ; and neither Betsy nor myself could shake Mr. Rigg 's conclusion . indeed , he become more and more emphatic , in reply to our doubt and mild suggestion , perhaps that his eye have deceive he , or perhaps that , take a nap in the corner of the buttress , he have dream at least a part of it . and Betsy , on the score of ancient friendship and kind remembrance of his liking , put it to he in a gentle way whether his knowledge of what Sally Mock have be , and the calumny she might have speak of his beer   when herself , in the work - house , deprive of it , might not have induce he to take a little more than usual in go down so deep for she . but he answer , " no ; it be nothing of the sort . deep he have go , to the tiptoe of his fling ; not from any feeling of a wish to keep she down , but just because the parish pay , and the parish would have measurement . and when that be on , he never bring down more than the quart tin from the public ; and never have none down afterward . otherwise the ground be so ticklish , that a man , work too free , might stay down there . no , no ! that idea be like one of Sally 's own . he just have his quart of Persfield ale short measure , of course , with a woman at the bar and if that be enough to make a man dream dream , the soon he dig his own grave , the well for all connect with he .  we see that we have go too far in thinking of such a possibility ; and if Mr. Rigg have not be large - minded , as well as notoriously sober , Betsy might have lose I all the benefit of his evidence by her London - breed clumsiness with he . for it take quite a different handling , and a different mode of outset , to get on with the London work class and the labor kind of the country ; or at least it seem to I so . 
 now my knowledge of Jacob Rigg be owe , as might be suppose , to Betsy Strouss , who have take the lead of I in almost every thing ever since I bring she down from London . and now I be glad that , in one point at least , her judgment have overrule mine to wit , that my name and parentage be as yet not generally know in the village . indeed , only Betsy herself and Jacob and a faithful old washer - woman , with no roof to her mouth , be aware of I as Miss Castlewood . not that I have take any other name to that I would not stoop but because the public , of its own accord , pay attention to Betsy 's style of address I , follow her lead   with some little improvement , and be pleased to entitle I " Miss Raumur .  some question have be raise as to spell I aright , till a man of advanced intelligence prove to many eye , and even several pair of spectacle   assemble in front of the blacksmith 's shop , that no other way could be right except that . for there it be in print , as any one able might see , on the side of an instrument whose name and quality be even more mysterious than those in debate . therefore I become " Miss Raumur ; " and a protest would have go for nothing unless print also . but it do not behoove I to go to that expense , while it suit I very well to be consider and pity as a harmless foreigner a be who on english land may find some cause to doubt whether , even in his own country , a prophet could be less thought of . and this large pity for I , as an outlandish person , in the very spot where I be bear , endow I with tenfold the privilege of the proud native . for the native of this valley be declare to be of a different stock from those around they , not of the common Wessex strain , but of Jutish or danish origin . how that may be I do not know ; at any rate , they think well of themselves , and no doubt they have cause to do so . 
 moreover , they all be very kind to I , and their primitive way amuse I , as soon as they have settle that I be a foreigner , equally beyond and below inquiry . they tell I that I be kindly welcome to stay there as long as it please I ; and know how fond I be of make picture , after behold my drawing - book , every farmer among they give I leave to come into his field , though he never have hear there be any thing there worth paint .

I tell he that if I be wrong in ask , he be not to answer ; but , right or wrong , ask he I must . 
 " the question be natural , and not at all improper , " reply Lord Castlewood , stand a moment for change of pain , which be all his relief . " indeed , I expect you to ask I that before . but , Erema , I have also have to ask myself about it , whether I have any right to answer you . and I have decide not to do so , unless you will pledge yourself to one thing . " 
 " I will pledge myself to any thing , " I answer , rashly ; " I do not care what it be , if only to get at the bottom of this mystery . " 
 " I scarcely think you will hold good to your word when you hear what you have to promise . the condition upon which I tell you what I believe to be the cause of all be , that you let thing remain as they be , and keep silence forever about they . "

 " after much consideration and strong wish to have it otherwise , I have conclude that I be so bound . " 
 " in duty to my father , or the family , or what ? forgive I for ask , but it do seem so hard . " 
 " it seem hard , my dear , and it be hard as well , " he answer , very gently , yet show in his eye and lip no chance of any yielding . " but remember that I do not know , I only guess , the secret ; and if you give the pledge I speak of , you merely follow in your father 's step . " 
 " never , " I reply , with as firm a face as his . " it may have be my father 's duty , or no doubt he think it so ; but it can not be mine , unless I make it so by lay it on my honor . and I will not do that . " 
 " perhaps you be right ; but , at any rate , remember that I have not try to persuade you . I wish to do what be for your happiness , Erema . and I think that , on the whole , with your vigor and high spirit , you be well as you be than if you have a knowledge which you could only brood over and not use . " 
 " I will find out the whole of it myself , " I cry , for I could not repress all excitement ; " and then I need not brood over it , but may have it out and get justice . in the wild part of America justice come with perseverance : be I to abjure it in the heart of England ? Lord Castlewood , which be first justice or honor ? " 
 " my cousin , you be fond of ask question difficult to answer . justice and honor nearly always go together . when they do otherwise , honor stand foremost , with people of good birth , at least . " 
 " then I will be a person of very bad birth . if they come into conflict in my life , as almost every thing seem to do , my first thought shall be of justice ; and honor shall come in as its ornament afterward . " 
 " Erema , " say my cousin , " your meaning be good , and at your time of life you can scarcely be expect to take a dispassionate view of thing . " 
 at first I feel almost as if I could hate a " dispassionate view of thing . " thing be make to arouse our passion , so long as meanness and villainy prevail ; and if old man , know the balance of the world , can contemplate they all " dispassionately , " more clearly than any thing else , to my mind , that prove the beauty of be young . I be sure that I never be hot or violent quality which I especially dislike but still I would rather almost have those than be too philosophical . and now , while I revere my father 's cousin for his gentleness , wisdom , and long - suffering , I almost long to fly back to the Major , prejudiced , peppery , and red - hot for justice , at any rate in all thing that concern himself .

 some answer to IT

" ' tis a good fight let they fight it out !  CHAPTER XL 

 the MAN AT last 

 this new alliance with Mrs. Busk not only refresh my courage , but help I forward most importantly . in truth , if it have not be for this I never could have bear what I have to bear , and meet the peril which I have to meet . for I have the confidence of feeling now that here be some one close at hand , an intelligent person , and well acquaint with the place and neighborhood , upon whom I could rely for warning , succor , and , if the bad should come to the very bad , revenge . it be true that already I have Jacob Rigg , and perhaps the protector promise by my cousin ; but the former be as ignorant as he be honest , and of the latter , as he make no sign , how could I tell any thing ?

 this phenomenon be beyond my gift of observation ; and know that my poor grandfather have scarcely set foot on the bridge , if ever he set foot there at all which at present be very doubtful also that he have fall backward , and only bleed internally , I could not reconcile tradition   however recent   with proven truth . and sure of no disturbance from the step of any native , here I often sit in a little bowered shelter of my own , well establish up the rise , down which the path make zigzag , and screen from that and the bridge as well by sheaf of twig and lop of leaf . it be a little forward thicket , quite detached from the upland copse , to which perhaps it have once belong , and crust up from the meadow slope with sod and mould in alternate step . and be quite the elbow of a foreland of the meadow - reach , it yield almost a " bird's - eye view " of the beautiful glade and the wandering brook .

I have often see those sweet thing that enjoy four leg walk in the line of distance as if they be no well off than we be , kindly desiring , perhaps , to make the biped spectator content with himself . and I be content to admire this cow or horse , or whatever it might be , without any more than could be help of that invidious feeling which have drive the human race now to establish its right to a tail , and its hope of four leg . so little , indeed , do I think of what I see , that when among the hazel twig , part carelessly by my hand , a cluster of nuts hung manifest , I gather it , and begin to crack and eat , although they be scarcely ripe yet . 
 but while employ in this pleasant way , I happen to glance again through my leafy screen , and then I distinguish the figure in the distance as that of a man walk rapidly . he be come down the mill - stream meadow toward the wooden bridge , carry a fishing rod , but clearly not intent on angle . for instead of follow the course of the stream , he be keep quite away from it , avoid also the footpath , or , at any rate , seem to prefer the long shadow of the tree and the tufte place . this make I look at he , and very soon I shrink into my nest and watch he . 
 as he come near any one could tell that he be no village workman , bold than the rest , and venturesome to cross the " murder - bridge " in his haste to be at home . the fishing rod alone be enough to show this when it come into clear view ; for our good people , though they fish sometimes , only use rough rod of their own making , without any varnish or brass thing for the line . and the man be of different height and walk and dress from any of our native .

" Ignoble as it be , and contemptible , and vile , and traitorous to all duty , my first thought be about my own escape ; for I feel that if this man see I there he would rush up the hill and murder I . within pistol - shot of the very place where my grandfather have be murder a lonely place , an unholy spot , and I be look at the hand that do it .

 no effort would compose or hush the heavy beating of my heart ; my lip be stiffen with dread of loud breath , and all power of motion leave I . for even a puff of wind might betray I , the ruffle of a spray , or the lifting of a leaf , or the random bounce of a beetle . great peril have encompass I ere now , but never have it grasp I as this do , and paralyze all the power of my body . rather would I have stand in the midst of a score of mexican rover than thus in the presence of that one man . and yet be not this the very thing for which I have wait , long , and labor ?  meanwhile the sunset glow be fail , and a gray autumnal haze creep up the tranquil valley . shadow wan and fade into dimness more diffuse , and light grow soft and vague and vaporous . the gleam of water , and the gloss of grass , and deep relief of tree , begin to lose their several phase and mingle into one large twilight blend . and cattle , from their milking shed , come low for more pasture ; and the bark of a shepherd 's dog rang quick , as if his sheep be drowsy .

 one part of his labor have not be describe , though doubtless a highly needful one . to erase the trace of his work , or at least obscure they to a careless eye , when he have turn as much ground as he think it worth his while to meddle with , he trod it back again to its level as nearly as might be , and then   with a can out of his fishing basket   sluice the place well with the water of the stream . this make it look to any heedless person , who would not descend to examine it , as if there have be nothing more than a little reflux from the river , cause by a flush from the mill - pond . this little stratagem increase my fear of a cunning and active villain . 




 CHAPTER XLI 

 a strong temptation

 " oh , do not   talk of they . they spoil every thing . and none of our people would say a word , or care to help we , if it come to that . the police be all stranger , and our people hate they . and , indeed , I believe that the bad thing ever do be the meddling of that old jobbin . the old stupe be still alive at Petersfield , and as pompous - headed as ever . my father would have be the man for your sad affair , miss , if the police have only be invent in his time . ah , yes , he be sharp ! not a Moonstock man you may take your oath of that , miss but a good honest native from Essex . but he marry my mother , a Moonstock woman ; or they would not put up with I here at all . you quality people have your idea to hold by , and despise all other , and reasonable in your opinion ; but you know nothing nothing nothing of the stiffness of the people under you . " 
 " how should I know any thing of that ? " I answer ; " all these thing be new to I . I have not be bring up in this country , as you know . I come from a large land , where your stiffness may have burst out into roughness , from have so much room suddenly . but tell I what you think now your father would have do in such a case as mine be . " 
 " Miss Erema , he be that long - headed that nobody could play leap - frog with he . none of they ever clear over his barrel . he walk into this village fifty - five year back , this very month , with his spade upon his shoulder and the knowledge of every body in his eye . they all put up against he , but they never put he down ; and in less than three month he go to church , I do assure you , with the only daughter of the only baker . after that he go into the baking line himself ; he turn his spade into a shovel , as he say , and he introduce new practice . " 
 " oh , Mrs. Busk , not adulteration ? " 
 " no , miss , no ! the very last thing he would think of . only the good use of potato in the bread , when flour be frightful bad and painful dear . what be the good meal of the day ? he use to reason . dinner . and why ? why , because of the potato . if I can make people take potato for their breakfast , and potato for their supper too , I be give they three meal a day instead of one . and the health of the village correspond to it . " 
 " oh , but , Mrs. Busk , he might have make they do it by persuasion , or at least with their own knowledge " 
 " no , miss , no ! the whole nature of our people , Moonstock or out of it , be never to take victual by any sort of persuasion . if St. Paul be to come and preach , ' eat this or that , ' all I have of it in the shop would go rotten . they hate any meddling with their liking , and they suspect doctor 's rubbish in all of it . " 
 " I be quite of their opinion , " I reply ; " and I be glad to hear of their independence . I always use to hear that in England none of the poor people dare have a will of their own . " 
 Mrs. Busk lift up her hand to express amazement at my ignorance , and say that she " must run away and put the shutter up , or else the policeman would come rap , and look for a glass of beer , which he have no right to till it come to the bottom of the firkin ; and this one be only tap last Sunday week . do not   you ever think of the police , miss . " 
 probably this be good advice , and it quite agree with the opinion of other , and my own impression as to the arrogant lethargy of " the force , " as they call themselves , in my father 's case . Mrs. Busk have more activity and intelligence in her little head than all the fat sergeant and inspector of the county , helmet , belt , and staff , and all . 




 CHAPTER XLII 

 MASTER withypool 

 at first I be much inclined to run for help , or at least for counsel , either to Lord Castlewood or to Major Hockin ; but further consideration keep I from do any thing of the kind . in the first place , neither of they would do much good ; for my cousin 's ill health would prevent he from help I , even if his strange view of the case do not , while the excellent Major be much too hot and hasty for a delicate task like this . and , again , I might lose the most valuable and important of all chance by be away from the spot just now . and so I remain at Shoxford for a while , keep strict watch upon the stranger 's haunt , and ask about he by mean of Mrs. Busk .

" he have be here only those three time this summer , upon excuse of fishing always . he stay at old Wellham , about five mile down the river , where the people be not true moonite . and one thing that puzzle they be , that although he put up there simply for the angling , he always choose time when the water be so low that to catch fish be next to impossible . he leave his fishing quarter upon the very day after you see he search so ; and he speak as if he do not mean to come again this season . and they say that they do not   want he neither , he be such a morose , close - fiste man ; and drink nothing but water , there be very little profit with he .
